    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4832463346117648434"><i>Death and the Dervish</i> is an acclaimed novel by Bosnian writer Mesa Selimovic. It recounts the story of Sheikh Nuruddin, a dervish residing in an Islamic monastery in Sarajevo in the eighteenth century during the Ottoman Turk hegemony over the Balkans. When his brother is arrested, he must descend into the Kafkaesque world of the Ottoman authorities in his search to discove</span>
  <span id="freeText4832463346117648434" style="display:none"><i>Death and the Dervish</i> is an acclaimed novel by Bosnian writer Mesa Selimovic. It recounts the story of Sheikh Nuruddin, a dervish residing in an Islamic monastery in Sarajevo in the eighteenth century during the Ottoman Turk hegemony over the Balkans. When his brother is arrested, he must descend into the Kafkaesque world of the Ottoman authorities in his search to discover what happened to him. He narrates his story in the form of an elaborate suicide note, regularly misquoting the Koran. In time, he begins to question his relations with society as a whole and, eventually, his life choices in general.<br /><br /> Hugely successful when published in the 1960s,<i> Death and the Dervish </i>is an enduring classic that was made into a feature length film in 1974.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4832463346117648434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2346878513" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2346878513" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2346878513?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2346878513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12014436528317469691">Guilt, law, justice: I’ve already read two grand books about law, justice and guilt – <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17690.The_Trial' title='The Trial by Franz Kafka' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Trial</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5223.Franz_Kafka' title='Franz Kafka' rel='nofollow noopener'>Franz Kafka</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/376561.Invitation_to_a_Beheading' title='Invitation to a Beheading by Vladimir Nabokov' rel='nofollow noopener'>Invitation to a Beheading</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5152.Vladimir_Nabokov' title='Vladimir Nabokov' rel='nofollow noopener'>Vladimir Nabokov</a> – and <i>Death and the Dervish</i> by <b>Meša Selimović</b> surely belongs in this fine row of masterpieces.<br/>The hero wants to pass his time in prayers and the passive contemplation of divinity and the world but to live is to take sides, and taking sides is dangerous…<br/><blockquote>I do not yet know what will be written here. But in the strokes of these letters at le</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText12014436528317469691" style="display:none">Guilt, law, justice: I’ve already read two grand books about law, justice and guilt – <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17690.The_Trial" title="The Trial by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Trial</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5223.Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">Franz Kafka</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/376561.Invitation_to_a_Beheading" title="Invitation to a Beheading by Vladimir Nabokov" rel="nofollow noopener">Invitation to a Beheading</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5152.Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov" rel="nofollow noopener">Vladimir Nabokov</a> – and <i>Death and the Dervish</i> by <b>Meša Selimović</b> surely belongs in this fine row of masterpieces.<br />The hero wants to pass his time in prayers and the passive contemplation of divinity and the world but to live is to take sides, and taking sides is dangerous…<br /><blockquote>I do not yet know what will be written here. But in the strokes of these letters at least some of what was in me will remain, no longer to perish in eddies of mist as if it had never been, or as if I had never known what happened. In this way I will come to see how I became what I am – this self that is a mystery even to me. And yet it is a mystery to me that I have not always been what I am now. I know these lines are muddled; my hand trembles at the task of disentanglement that I face, at the trial I now commence. Here I am everything: judge, witness, and accused.</blockquote><br />Everything that happens is ambiguous and ambivalent… Justice belongs to those who wield power… Culprits are those whom the endued with power name guilty… <br /><blockquote>“If you don’t think blackly, things can get blacker. Nothing depends on you. It doesn’t help to be either brave or cowardly, neither to curse nor to weep; nothing can help you. So sit and wait for your lot, and it’s already black since you’re here. That’s what I think: if you’re not guilty, then it’s their mistake. If you are guilty, then it’s your mistake. If you’re innocent, then misfortune has struck you, as if you’ve fallen into a deep whirlpool. And if you’re not innocent, you’ve earned it, nothing more.”</blockquote><br />And the moral is the most ambiguous of all things. There are plenty levels of morality for everyone… Moral is layered like onion skin…<br /><blockquote>They dreamed of rulers who were good, but who was that? As far as he was concerned, he dreamed of bribable ones, he liked them the most because there was a way to them. Worst are the honest ones, who need nothing, who have no human weaknesses, and know only about some higher law, which is almost incomprehensible to ordinary men. No one can do more evil than they can. They create enough hatred to last for a hundred years.</blockquote><br />He who walks the path of moral compromises inevitably turns into an instrument of evil.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12014436528317469691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Lars K Jensen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4178860-lars-k-jensen"><img alt="Lars K Jensen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502272390p2/4178860.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/519818966?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lars K Jensen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lars K Jensen" href="/user/show/4178860-lars-k-jensen">Lars K Jensen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4178860-lars-k-jensen?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer519818966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17650776820173118825">Imagine a bag of candy with 455 pieces of the most delicious pieces of candy inside. Each is so delicate and mouth-watering that you feel the need to expand the joy to as many minutes as possible, and it sweetens the soul, just like it does the tongue.<br/><br/>This is basically what this book was like to me. It's not an easy read (and thank God for that), but it's great literature. Such great literature.<br/><br/>The book (written 1962-66 and a classic in Yugoslav literature) is set in Sarajevo under Ottoman rule </span>
  <span id="freeText17650776820173118825" style="display:none">Imagine a bag of candy with 455 pieces of the most delicious pieces of candy inside. Each is so delicate and mouth-watering that you feel the need to expand the joy to as many minutes as possible, and it sweetens the soul, just like it does the tongue.<br /><br />This is basically what this book was like to me. It's not an easy read (and thank God for that), but it's great literature. Such great literature.<br /><br />The book (written 1962-66 and a classic in Yugoslav literature) is set in Sarajevo under Ottoman rule (18th century) and has often been compared to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17690.The_Trial" title="The Trial by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Trial</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5223.Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">Franz Kafka</a>, which is quite obvious when you think of the plot (man vs. system). I would, personally, describe it as a combination of both The Trial and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7144.Crime_and_Punishment_" title="Crime and Punishment  by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Crime and Punishment</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>, based on the fact that the protagonist has long conversations with himself, mostly on doubt and indecisiveness. It's so riveting.<br /><br />The book is incredibly slow in its storyteling, so if you haven't got the patience (or attention span) for that, I pity you - and think you should read something else. Maybe a magazine, or the subtitles to a Hollywood action movie.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17650776820173118825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2509138408" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12803074148103998106">Real Rating: 3.5* of five<br/><br/>An intense read. Beautiful translated words confronting and comforting the human fear of love by the means of examining the easier-to-grasp fear of death.<br/><br/>I've chosen some favorite phrases and liked them from the quotes. They appear below the review. I think the patience required to read a footnoted and glossary'd read won't allow its subtleties and tremendous pleasures to spread widely among US English-speaking readers.<br/><br/>Make no mistake: Ahmed Nuruddin is you, reader, a ma</span>
  <span id="freeText12803074148103998106" style="display:none">Real Rating: 3.5* of five<br /><br />An intense read. Beautiful translated words confronting and comforting the human fear of love by the means of examining the easier-to-grasp fear of death.<br /><br />I've chosen some favorite phrases and liked them from the quotes. They appear below the review. I think the patience required to read a footnoted and glossary'd read won't allow its subtleties and tremendous pleasures to spread widely among US English-speaking readers.<br /><br />Make no mistake: Ahmed Nuruddin is you, reader, a man whose loves and One True Love don't mark him out from the herd but place him in the center of it. He doesn't do a single thing that any one of us couldn't do, be it generous or cowardly or divinely inspired. It is simply that we wouldn't tread in his footsteps, wouldn't elect to give ourselves to an ideal in a world without respect for them.<br /><br />I give the book a paltry rating, based on those comments; I found it necessary to break my reading into smaller bites than I would have liked to do. The feast is so rich, satisfying my word-lust in such short order, that I ended up feeling disconnected by the enforced consumption of many amuse-bouche reads between this book's courses.<br /><br />I suspect many readers will be defeated by that very need. This is a book that, due to its own delights, ends up unfinished, bookmark poking the piles of paperbacks athwart its spine, a guilty glance sliding past it as the New Year's Resolution cry "I WILL FINISH THIS SHELF!" rings its falsely sincere echoes into our shared shame.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12803074148103998106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer783277847719795359"> ENGLISH <br/><br/><b>

  <span id="freeText783277847719795359" style="display:none"> ENGLISH <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"People despise everyone who succeeds and hate the ones who surpass them. Get used to scorn if you want peace and hate if you agree to fight"</i>
</b><br /><br /> BOSNIAN <br /><br /><i>“Ljudi preziru sve one koji ne uspiju, a mrze one koji se uspnu iznad njih; Navikni se na prezir ako želis mir, ili na mržnju ako pristaneš na borbu...” </i><br />_________________________<br /><br />I read this book back in high school and it was a school assignment. The writer of this book is from the city where I went to school. Our teacher urged us to read this book, not because it was a school assignment. She said this book makes you think. And I read it and it was beautiful, beyond beautiful.<br />_________________________<br /><br /> ENGLISH <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"Thousand times you regret what you have said, rarely what you have not."</i>
</b><br /><br /> BOSNIAN <br /><br /><i>“Hiljadu puta se pokaješ za ono što kažeš, rijetko za ono što prećutiš.”</i><br />_________________________<br /><br />The writing style is complicated and not easy to read. But these quotations show you what a masterpiece this book is. I wish this book was more appreciated in the world. It is full of wisdom. I want to find an english version of this book. Sadly, this author is not well known outside the ex-Yugoslavian countries.<br />__________________________<br /> ENGLISH <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"Lonely, in between the graves, I forgot hate. It came back when I approached people."</i>
</b><br /><br /> BOSNIAN<br /><br /><i>“Usamljen među grobovima, zaboravio sam na mržnju. Vratila mi se kad sam prišao ljudima.” </i><br />___________________________<br /><i>VERY IMPORTANT NOTE: I translated the quotations from Bosnian to English myself. Sorry for all the mistakes that I made.</i><br /><br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/146296394?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca Duncan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca Duncan" href="/user/show/4932823-rebecca-duncan">Rebecca Duncan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788661070181" href="/book/show/8487092-dervi-i-smrt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146296394" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13037707887693847540">This book is a huge favorite of mine and I'm quite surprised that I haven't written about it - yet.<br/><br/>But I'm here now to correct that and to pay homage to this unprecedented masterpiece of, I dare to say, world literature.<br/>When I first came across this book, I was frankly very, very, unimaginably young and even more so immature. But, even at that period in my life, I din't fail to recognize that this novel is like a rare diamond that not many people are allowed to discover,understand and accept.<br/><br/>Not</span>
  <span id="freeText13037707887693847540" style="display:none">This book is a huge favorite of mine and I'm quite surprised that I haven't written about it - yet.<br /><br />But I'm here now to correct that and to pay homage to this unprecedented masterpiece of, I dare to say, world literature.<br />When I first came across this book, I was frankly very, very, unimaginably young and even more so immature. But, even at that period in my life, I din't fail to recognize that this novel is like a rare diamond that not many people are allowed to discover,understand and accept.<br /><br />Not knowing where to start,and afraid I maybe won't transfer what lies in the depths of the wisdom that this book brings and gives, I would like to say what the novel is about in a couple of unpretentious sentences. <br /><br />Basically, it is a quest for Justice, but the quest is doomed to fail at its very beginning. The quest is taken by a man who is not your ordinary man, but a person who lives under very strict rules of moral and divine providence, and he can never escape from what he is.<br />He is searching for a way to free his brother who is trapped in a tower because of some (political) offence he did. Little does the hero know that the verdict has been reached in advance, and that there is no way out not only for his lost brother, but, even more so, for him. <br /><br /><br />The novel is written in the "I - form", and the author is very skilled in connecting, contrasting, explaining and hiding his feelings through his writing, and the reader doesn't fail to utterly connect with the authors dilemmas and his enormous sadness and pain. The book speaks not only of Justice, or the nonexistence of the latter, but also of Life and Love and People and Man, and Truth and Lie, and it goes on ad infinitum.<br /><br />It always seemed to me that the author writes his thoughts and makes them into sentences with such ease and fluency, and I read this text with awe even today, nearly ten years after reading it for the first time as a very silly girl. To this day, when I'm feeling perplexed about life and my existence and all those, to quote the Pythons, "meaning of life" questions, I just read a couple of sentences from here, and I immediately feel my confusion fading.<br />I'm grateful for that, and i think that this is definitely a must - read novel!<br /><br />Start today :)<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="13037707887693847540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12710311523173945119" style="display:none">All we try to do in life is have a congruent and balanced view about the world, people and what's happening around us. We believe in that we live in a just world, suffering people suffer because they must have done something to deserve this (!) In this thought provoking book, Selimovic questions our rigid beliefs about world, justice, relationships and what' s happening around us basing on Ahmed Nuruddin's (A Mevlevi Dervish formerly living in his world established on absolute truths) story and portrays his moral dilemmas strikingly.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8872533655249863232">My closest Bosnian friend gave this to me after I'd read Ivo Andric's Bridge on the Drina. This is not as accessible a book as Andric's masterpiece. Drawing a comparison, Andric is Tolstoy while Selimović is Dostoevsky. While Andric gives you everything you need to know to appreciate his book, Selimović presupposes the reader knows enough to fill in the background.<br/> <br/></span>

<div id="review_2061796232" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2061796232" />
    <a title="John Farebrother" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68609816-john-farebrother"><img alt="John Farebrother" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499497213p2/68609816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2061796232?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Farebrother" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Farebrother" href="/user/show/68609816-john-farebrother">John Farebrother</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788661070181" href="/book/show/8487092-dervi-i-smrt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2061796232" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4496979178978507824">Another classic Yugoslav novel, by former Partisan and Bosnian Muslim Selimović. This book explores many of the same themes as his next novel, also a classic, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8487090.Tvr_ava' title='Tvrđava by Meša Selimović' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tvrđava</a>. Set in the Middle Ages, the novel dissects and exposes the nature of totalitarian regimes. A former fighter, become a priest, is drawn against his will into the political intrigues of the Ottoman Balkans when his brother is arrested for speaking out against injustice. Hamlet-like, the other-wordly priest finds himself obliged to a</span>
  <span id="freeText4496979178978507824" style="display:none">Another classic Yugoslav novel, by former Partisan and Bosnian Muslim Selimović. This book explores many of the same themes as his next novel, also a classic, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8487090.Tvr_ava" title="Tvrđava by Meša Selimović" rel="nofollow noopener">Tvrđava</a>. Set in the Middle Ages, the novel dissects and exposes the nature of totalitarian regimes. A former fighter, become a priest, is drawn against his will into the political intrigues of the Ottoman Balkans when his brother is arrested for speaking out against injustice. Hamlet-like, the other-wordly priest finds himself obliged to act, and casting off his pious innocence finds himself stooping to the level of his enemies in order to confound them. This he succeeds in doing in spectacular fashion; only to discover that he is now in a trap of his own making. For those who rise in the hierarchy of totalitarian regimes end up as victims themselves, and what they thought was their route to freedom only results in ever more slavery to a ruthless and inhuman system that inexorably dehumanises and ultimately destroys its proponents. A must read for anyone with an interest in communism, and the Balkans.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4496979178978507824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2061813737" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2061813737" />
    <a title="Jovan Autonomašević" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69438797-jovan-autonoma-evi"><img alt="Jovan Autonomašević" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500034872p2/69438797.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2061813737?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jovan Autonomašević" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jovan Autonomašević" href="/user/show/69438797-jovan-autonoma-evi">Jovan Autonomašević</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788661070181" href="/book/show/8487092-dervi-i-smrt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2061813737" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2123820307823039746">An epic Balkan book. What is it about? As in his other masterpiece, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35619415.Tvr_ava' title='Tvrđava by Meša Selimović' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tvrđava</a>, Selimović posits the issues of his time (post-WWII Yugoslavia) in a more distant past, which allows him to not only cast those issues in a more objective light, but also to explore that little-known past and bring it to life. The main issues dealt with by the book are injustice, and how individuals operate in a totalitarian regime: the plots, manipulations and kickbacks that are necessary to get anything done. Like the </span>
  <span id="freeText2123820307823039746" style="display:none">An epic Balkan book. What is it about? As in his other masterpiece, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35619415.Tvr_ava" title="Tvrđava by Meša Selimović" rel="nofollow noopener">Tvrđava</a>, Selimović posits the issues of his time (post-WWII Yugoslavia) in a more distant past, which allows him to not only cast those issues in a more objective light, but also to explore that little-known past and bring it to life. The main issues dealt with by the book are injustice, and how individuals operate in a totalitarian regime: the plots, manipulations and kickbacks that are necessary to get anything done. Like the hero of the book (a Muslim mystic), the author's own brother was executed unjustly (summarily for theft during WWII, despite the author's intercessions). Selimović also recreates medieval Bosnia in a manner that cannot fail to fascinate anyone familiar with the Balkans. The result is a vivid portrayal of Balkan totalitarianism, where religion is a smokescreen for the exercise of absolute power, but where there are also limits to the impositions that ordinary people will tolerate.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2123820307823039746" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_976415671" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/976415671" />
    <a title="Paul Fulcher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3250759-paul-fulcher"><img alt="Paul Fulcher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542271295p2/3250759.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/976415671?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Fulcher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Fulcher" href="/user/show/3250759-paul-fulcher">Paul Fulcher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3250759-paul-fulcher?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer976415671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18276749492844061055">This is why I love translated fiction: a book written in the 1960s, which took 30 years to be translated into English and has languished in relative obscurity since, but an outstanding read.<br/><br/>The plot of the Death and the Dervish is relatively straightforward. The narrator, a Dervish living in Ottoman-ruled Bosnia, has his life turned upside down when his brother is arrested. The novel tells of his Kafkesque struggles with the authorities to discover of what his brother id accused, his desparate a</span>
  <span id="freeText18276749492844061055" style="display:none">This is why I love translated fiction: a book written in the 1960s, which took 30 years to be translated into English and has languished in relative obscurity since, but an outstanding read.<br /><br />The plot of the Death and the Dervish is relatively straightforward. The narrator, a Dervish living in Ottoman-ruled Bosnia, has his life turned upside down when his brother is arrested. The novel tells of his Kafkesque struggles with the authorities to discover of what his brother id accused, his desparate attempts to save him, his revenge when he fails to do so, and how this ultimately harms both him and his friends. While set in the c17th Century (no date is ever given) Ottoman Empire, the plot is actually based on an incident from the author's own life in Post-WW2 Communist Yugoslavia.<br /><br />However, the key interest of the novel - told as a first-person interior monologue - is primarily in the physcology and philosophy of the narrator.<br /><br />We follow the journey of his life through three phases, firstly as an idealistic young soldier, then, after his chosen love gets married whilst he is away on military duty, his withdrawal from the world as a deeply religious Muslim ascetic. <br /><br />However, following his brother's arrest he decides that he cannot remain detached from the world and must get involved in the politics and intrigues of both the Empire and organised religious life. Hatred and revenge become his new guiding force.<br /><br />"<i>I wish you eternal piece, you inexperienced young man of long ago, who burned with pure flames and a need for sacrifice. And I wish eternal piece to you, honorable and noble Sheikh-Nuruddin, who believed in the power of gentleness and God's word. I light a candle to each of you in my memory and in my heart, you who were kind and naïve.</i>"<br /><br />This realisation comes to him only very gradually, and this part of the book is deliberately very drawn out - indeed the narrator admits:<br /><br />"<i>A reader with good intentions might tell me: you're dragging things out too much; you're philosophising too much. I would respond immediately: I know. I'm spinnig this meager thought out to a great length, shaking it like an empty jug when you cannot get another drop out of it. But I am doing that on purpose, to delay telling what upsets me even today, several months after it all happened. However, beating around the bush will not help, I cannot avoid it, and I will not stop it.</i>"<br /><br />The latter part - as he comes to realise, in both senses of the word, the personal compromises required for involvement in political life and the unsecure nature of being in power - is told at a deliberately much faster pace and becomes almost thrilleresque.<br /><br />The book also gives, indirectly, a fascinating insight into Ottoman Bosnia, a world where e.g. Christianity is treated as an old, pagan, religion, as well as 20th Century Yugoslavia.<br /><br />And the 30 year wait for the translation was worth it, given the beautiful effort by Bogdan Rakic and Stephen Dickey.<br /><br />Strongly recommended.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18276749492844061055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3036688010" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3036688010" />
    <a title="Sotiris Karaiskos" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18940246-sotiris-karaiskos"><img alt="Sotiris Karaiskos" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407195235p2/18940246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3036688010?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sotiris Karaiskos" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sotiris Karaiskos" href="/user/show/18940246-sotiris-karaiskos">Sotiris Karaiskos</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/30966773">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18940246-sotiris-karaiskos?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3036688010" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2607397515601431875">One of these novels contains many more things than a simple storytelling. The author is inspired by the death of his brother to write a story about the Ottoman era in Bosnia to speak more confidently about what he lived in communist Yugoslavia. The protagonist of the story learns that his brother is accused of a very serious crime and is trying to figure out what is happening the maze of secular and religious bureaucracy, in a wand reminiscent of Kafka's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17690.The_Trial' title='The Trial by Franz Kafka' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Trial</a>, perhaps in a more explicit for</span>
  <span id="freeText2607397515601431875" style="display:none">One of these novels contains many more things than a simple storytelling. The author is inspired by the death of his brother to write a story about the Ottoman era in Bosnia to speak more confidently about what he lived in communist Yugoslavia. The protagonist of the story learns that his brother is accused of a very serious crime and is trying to figure out what is happening the maze of secular and religious bureaucracy, in a wand reminiscent of Kafka's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17690.The_Trial" title="The Trial by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Trial</a>, perhaps in a more explicit form. The protagonist's anxiety is particularly intense, apparently reflecting the anxiety of the author, leading to thoughts about human justice, punishment and forgiveness, our duties within society, the power of authority and its limits and much more things.<br /><br />This completeness and the multitude of meanings is what gives this book its value. At some point, the reader may be confused by all the chaos that is being described, but apparently this is within the author's intentions to give the best impression of the irrational structure of society beneath the seeming order. This makes reading a little difficult, not so much in understanding the text, as it is simple enough, as in trying to understand what is behind it, but because I firmly believe that it is important for literature to put us in thought and make it difficult for us to this is a book that does just that. So after a bit of an internal struggle, I'll give the top rate.<br /><br /> Ένα από αυτά τα μυθιστορήματα που περιέχουν πολλά περισσότερα πράγματα από μία απλή αφήγηση μιας ιστορίας. Ο συγγραφέας εμπνέεται από το θάνατο του αδερφού του για να γράψει μία ιστορία που εκτυλίσσεται στη Βοσνία της οθωμανικής περιόδου και μέσα από αυτή να μιλήσει με μεγαλύτερη ασφάλεια για αυτά που ζούσε στην κομμουνιστική Γιουγκοσλαβία. Ο πρωταγωνιστής της ιστορίας μαθαίνει ότι ο αδερφός του κατηγορείται για ένα πολύ σοβαρό έγκλημα και προσπαθεί να βγάλει άκρη μέσα στον λαβύρινθο της κοσμικής και θρησκευτικής γραφειοκρατίας, σε μία περιπλάνηση που θυμίζει την Δική του Κάφκα, ίσως σε μία πιο ξεκάθαρη μορφή. Η αγωνία του πρωταγωνιστή είναι ιδιαίτερα έντονη, αντικατοπτρίζοντας προφανώς την αντίστοιχη του συγγραφέα, και οδηγεί σε σκέψεις για τη δικαιοσύνη των ανθρώπων, την τιμωρία και τη συγχώρεση, τα καθήκοντά μας μέσα στην κοινωνία, την δύναμη της εξουσίας και τα όριά της και για πολλά άλλα πράγματα.<br /><br /> Αυτή η πληρότητα και το πλήθος των νοημάτων είναι που δίνει αξία σε αυτό το βιβλίο. Σε κάποια σημεία ίσως ο αναγνώστης μπερδευτεί από όλο αυτό το χάος που περιγράφεται αλλά προφανώς κάτι τέτοιο είναι μέσα στις προθέσεις του συγγραφέα για να δώσει με το καλύτερο τρόπο την εντύπωση από την παράλογη δομή της κοινωνίας κάτω από την τάξη που φαινομενικά υπάρχει. Αυτό κάνει την ανάγνωση λίγο δύσκολη, όχι τόσο στο επίπεδο της κατανόησης του κειμένου που είναι όσο απλό χρειάζεται, όσο στην προσπάθεια κατανόησης αυτού που κρύβεται από πίσω αλλά επειδή πιστεύω ακράδαντα ότι είναι σημαντικό η λογοτεχνία να μας βάζει σε σκέψεις και γενικότερα να μας δυσκολεύει και αυτό είναι ένα βιβλίο που κάνει αυτό ακριβώς το πράγμα. Οπότε μετά από λίγη εσωτερική πάλη θα βάλω την άριστη βαθμολογία.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2607397515601431875" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8131853990768597626" style="display:none">A book about self analysis. A book about sacrifice. A book about life, religion, feelings and the impact of the society on a religious figure not really able to adapt and become what was expected of him...just another slave to his consciousness. <br /><br />This story is a powerful psychological drama, filled with philosophical view of the world. <br /><br />Ahmed Nurudin, a derviš (poor muslim ascet), must find a way to cope with the world that's surrounding him...he must try to explain to himself the arrest of his brother and the feelings the event has brought. Nurudin must learn how to combine the religious muslim dogmas with his own love and loyalty to his brother while trying to justify to himself the strict totalitarian ideology of the 19th century Bosnia. Nurudin will learn that suffering and fear are an inevitable part of everyday life. <br /><br />Highly recommended. </span>

<div id="review_388672515" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2270963774" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2270963774" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9809633-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482968499p2/9809633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2270963774?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/9809633-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2270963774" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15178356829693290828">Death and the Dervish has been likened to the works of Kafka, and certainly it has a few similarities – its uncertainty of time and location (we never learn in exactly which town or historical period the story is taking place, only that it occurred at some point in the past in Yugoslavia) and in its portrayal of a sinister controlling organisation that seemingly doles out its punishments randomly.<br/><br/>I was reminded of another writer of East European origin, Joseph Conrad. Early in the story, the pro</span>
  <span id="freeText15178356829693290828" style="display:none">Death and the Dervish has been likened to the works of Kafka, and certainly it has a few similarities – its uncertainty of time and location (we never learn in exactly which town or historical period the story is taking place, only that it occurred at some point in the past in Yugoslavia) and in its portrayal of a sinister controlling organisation that seemingly doles out its punishments randomly.<br /><br />I was reminded of another writer of East European origin, Joseph Conrad. Early in the story, the protagonist Ahmed Nuruddin, a member of an Islamic holy order, witnesses a man running from mysterious assailants whom we assume are government soldiers, and Nuruddin faces a dilemma about whether to shelter this stranger or hand him over to the authorities.<br /><br />A similar situation arises in two stories by Joseph Conrad. In Conrad’s short story, ‘The Secret Sharer’ the Captain sides with the fugitive and protects him from capture, even though he knows that the man has committed a murder. In Conrad’s novel, Under Western Eyes, Razumov is asked by an assassin to give him shelter, but the troubled Russian instead chooses to turn him over to the authorities<br /><br />The outcomes are opposite, but they share one thing – the hero makes a firm decision, right or wrong, and deals with the consequences of that choice. The dilemma of Nuruddin is more nebulous. He is uncertain whether or not his fugitive is innocent, but Nuruddin fails to make any clear choice about what to do with him. He allows the intruder to stay for one night, but then reports the matter to another member of his tekke (the building in which his religious order lives), and leaves that man to report the fugitive, who by that time has left.<br /><br />This incident is not what the story of Death and the Dervish is about, but it is emblematic of the behaviour of its hero throughout the entire novel. Nuruddin continually prevaricates between decisions, never entirely making a choice. It is hard to tell whether his choices would have improved matters. Perhaps they would have made things worse. However Nuruddin lacks the moral courage to take a firm principled stand when it matters.<br /><br />The story is propelled by a number of similar instances. Nuruddin’s brother has been arrested and it is his duty to seek justice for his brother, and try to get him released. However while Nuruddin makes a few dutiful attempts to help his brother, his efforts are lukewarm, and he fails to prevent his brother from dying in prison.<br /><br />Indeed that his brother was sent to prison at all is the fault of Nuruddin. During his time as a soldier, Nuruddin stood by while a prostitute was executed by soldiers for siding with the enemy. He attempted to help the woman’s son by raising him in the tekke, but he fails to offer enough love to the unfortunate boy, and the boy retaliates by reporting Nuruddin’s brother to the authorities.<br /><br />The death of Nuruddin’s brother finally spurs some real emotion and action from this pusillanimous man, as he is moved to feel hatred for those responsible for his brother’s death. He had not shown much love or concern for his brother in life, but his failure to save his brother does produce some stirring in him, perhaps guilt.<br /><br />It is not clear how Nuruddin reconciles his hatred with his religion. Perhaps he does not even try. Certainly he seems to forget his faith for quite long periods, and many of his supposed quotations from the Qu’ran are at best misquoted, and at worst not in the Qu’ran at all. Certainly prayer and faith in the goodness of god have offered him little protection so far.<br /><br />Hatred finally inspires Nuruddin to conspire against the authorities who put his brother to death, and his schemes bear fruit when the legal official responsible is murdered after a mob are stirred up by another false accusation. Nuruddin is the main beneficiary of the revolt, as he now becomes a senior official in the town. However it is hard to feel sympathy or approval for his actions in seeking revenge, because they follow such meagre attempts at saving his brother beforehand.<br /><br />In any case, Nuruddin’s personality has not changed as much as all that. When his friend Hassan faces arrest, Nuruddin once more fails to act decisively to protect Hassan, fearing retribution from the authorities. He signs the order for Hassan’s arrest, but when Hassan escapes, he takes both the credit and blame for this deed, and he is executed. It is appropriate that his execution is for the heroic deed he should have done, and not for the cautious and half-hearted action that he did take.<br /><br />The defence that Nuruddin gives for his actions or lack of them is that choices are uncertain, and the outcome might not have been any better if he had chosen another route. This seems like mere excuse-making, and to some extent it is. Similarly the book is narrated in a dense and philosophical manner by Nuruddin that seems designed only to lose sight of his indecision amidst the forest of words.<br /><br />Yet there are points to be made in favour of Nuruddin. Perhaps his behaviour is closer to that of the majority of human nature, more than we like to admit. Perhaps most of us are making our choices in life, paralysed by the fear of making the wrong choice, and doomed to regret any choice that we do make. Maybe there are no good choices, and our actions are often doomed to disappointment and failure.<br /><br />Nuruddin’s choices are also dictated to him by an oppressive governmental structure and belief system. It seems unlikely that he could have saved his brother in any case, because to do so would be to question the moral legitimacy of governance. If governance is god-given, it cannot be wrong. If it not god-given, then how can there be governance? Ruled by this simplistic attitude towards the law, the state has no choice but to pursue its punishment of anyone accused.<br /><br />I am reminded of the way in which the Soviet Union put dissidents in mental asylums because the state would be conceding that it was not perfect if it admitted that there were sane people out there who might oppose it. <br /><br />Indeed the book was inspired by the arrest of Selimovic’s own brother by the Communist state, and it is not too far-fetched to assume that he was intending some of his critique in the book to be addressed at Communism. This may explain the vagueness of time and location, thereby ensuring that his attack was veiled enough to keep himself safe. It may even explain why Nuruddin is so unsympathetic a character – perhaps Selimovic is blaming himself for what happened to his brother, and projecting his guilt and anger against himself onto his hero.<br /><br />Is there any solution to the problems caused by action and inaction? None is clearly voiced, but there are a few hints. Nuruddin’s problem is that he has failed to act out of love. His rejection of the prostitute’s son, his timidity in helping his brother and his betrayal of his friend are all occasions when Nuruddin has betrayed ties of affection and isolated himself from other people.<br /><br />A more gentle philosophy is outlined by Hassan, the wisest character in the book. He proposes that we should not ‘plan’ our kindness to others as this allows for things to go wrong. Our good deeds should arise as spontaneously as possible, and here may be where our salvation lies.<br /><br />Death and the Dervish is densely written, and its meaning is frequently illusive. I am not even certain that I have fully grasped the mystery of the book in this review. However it is a fascinating and detached glimpse of a series of moral problems as presented in a world where the lawgivers themselves have failed to provide wisdom and morality.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15178356829693290828" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_584907988" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/584907988" />
    <a title="Larry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7349367-larry"><img alt="Larry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336174857p2/7349367.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/584907988?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larry" href="/user/show/7349367-larry">Larry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7349367-larry?shelf=male-fiction">male-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer584907988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13513134695954804661">This was a difficult book to read. Not because the author has no skills. Not because the storyline is boring or confusing. At it's best, which is a great deal of the time, the book is lucid, interesting, compelling even, and easily relatable for the reader. Written almost entirely in the first person of the main character, it is often like walking around inside someone's head, listening to them sharing their thoughts with you, and occasionally just slipping off to talk only to themselves. And th</span>
  <span id="freeText13513134695954804661" style="display:none">This was a difficult book to read. Not because the author has no skills. Not because the storyline is boring or confusing. At it's best, which is a great deal of the time, the book is lucid, interesting, compelling even, and easily relatable for the reader. Written almost entirely in the first person of the main character, it is often like walking around inside someone's head, listening to them sharing their thoughts with you, and occasionally just slipping off to talk only to themselves. And that is when the problems with the narrative develop. We, as humans, sometimes have quite chaotic thoughts as we sort through what we are absorbing. Ideas bounce around seeking some clarity. In this book's case, the character's search for clarity seems to derive directly from the author's own search for clarity, making the chaos real, not manufactured by the author. There is even a rare portion of the book where the author gets so imbedded into the character describing another character that the narrative shifts fully from first person to third person. Then, as though the author comes out of his own lost thoughts, the narrative returns as before. There is a great deal of insight to human behavior in this book, both personal and societal behavior, making it a solid candidate for a repeat reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13513134695954804661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2216872233" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2216872233" />
    <a title="Wulf" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62825595-wulf"><img alt="Wulf" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482536296p2/62825595.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2216872233?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wulf" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wulf" href="/user/show/62825595-wulf">Wulf</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783701308378" href="/book/show/1679498.Der_Derwisch_und_der_Tod">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62825595-wulf?shelf=novel-roman-no-genre">novel-roman-no-genre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62825595-wulf?shelf=setting-balkans-and-anatolia">setting-balkans-and-anatolia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62825595-wulf?shelf=topic-faith">topic-faith</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2216872233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4712841027152001468">You’ve made it: After a rather wild youth you have settled down as a dervish in the Ottoman Empire, in a comfy little tekije in what today is Bosnia. From this abode now and then you venture forth into town to visit the faithful, always a thoughtful Quran quote on your lips – a well-respected, wise man in the eyes of townsfolk and authorities alike. <br/><br/>But then your brother gets into trouble: Shouldn’t you use your influence to bail him out? You hem and haw, you question your motives, suffer pangs </span>
  <span id="freeText4712841027152001468" style="display:none">You’ve made it: After a rather wild youth you have settled down as a dervish in the Ottoman Empire, in a comfy little tekije in what today is Bosnia. From this abode now and then you venture forth into town to visit the faithful, always a thoughtful Quran quote on your lips – a well-respected, wise man in the eyes of townsfolk and authorities alike. <br /><br />But then your brother gets into trouble: Shouldn’t you use your influence to bail him out? You hem and haw, you question your motives, suffer pangs of conscience – and finally decide to act, to approach those in positions of power, who will surely help you out in your hour of need. Embarrassing, yet what can you do?<br /><br />And then you realize that the respect you have always commanded as a sort of pious mascot is gone; once you ask people to actually do something for you, they try their best to ignore you or lecture you on your insufficient understanding. Slowly, you find something giving way inside you, the vacillator turning into a rebellious schemer. The town is getting restless and you are stoking the unrest without quite realizing what it is that you are doing. And then things change big time with you at the very heart of the upheaval – or do they?<br /><br />Mind you, prospective reader, <i>Death and the Dervish</i> is a slow read, the protagonist forever (and sometimes hilariously) second-guessing each and every move he makes, with a welcome change of pace towards the end and a turning point that caught me by surprise. It is also perceptive, assiduously mapping the heart and mind of an introspective character who would contemplate life rather than act decisively. Most of all, it is just beautifully written, with a host of passages you’ll want to read again and again: lovers of elaborately crafted language will feel their mouths water just reading the first page. <br /><br />It’s one of those hidden gems I would never have come across if it hadn’t been for Goodreads and its lists; that’s what makes this site so great.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4712841027152001468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1424419729" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1424419729" />
    <a title="TJ" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43033167-tj"><img alt="TJ" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446563139p2/43033167.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1424419729?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TJ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TJ" href="/user/show/43033167-tj">TJ</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43033167-tj?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1424419729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6223092653306989610">As a person who typically salivates all over fragmented novels with eclectic content, this one took me by surprise. It just felt so singular. Like one of those impressive slab tables made from a single enormous piece of wood. I know that sounds incredibly unremarkable, I guess a more impressive comparison would be a marble statue. <br/>Well, here's a quote from the introduction that hits the nail on the head: "Death and the Dervish is a hard book to extract freestanding passages from; virtually every</span>
  <span id="freeText6223092653306989610" style="display:none">As a person who typically salivates all over fragmented novels with eclectic content, this one took me by surprise. It just felt so singular. Like one of those impressive slab tables made from a single enormous piece of wood. I know that sounds incredibly unremarkable, I guess a more impressive comparison would be a marble statue. <br />Well, here's a quote from the introduction that hits the nail on the head: "Death and the Dervish is a hard book to extract freestanding passages from; virtually every line and paragraph in the book derive their power not so much from the charge they carry within themselves as from their inextricable relationship to everything that precedes and follows them." The prose is concise, but very beautiful and continuously profound. <br />To keep it short: its one of those books where at like page 200, you would trust the narrator with your children and email password, by then when you finish you feel like you've never been more betrayed. Very emotional and spiritually harrowing book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6223092653306989610" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_462563360" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/462563360" />
    <a title="Jonathan yates" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2374445-jonathan-yates"><img alt="Jonathan yates" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375770516p2/2374445.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/462563360?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan yates" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan yates" href="/user/show/2374445-jonathan-yates">Jonathan yates</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer462563360" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17645361032852961063">The reason to read this book is pretty clear, first its beautiful, its a slow meditative beautiful story of a fictional past which for me is also a world far away, each page is slow and the details are full of color and light, sighing and the distant sounds of brooks<br/>but secondly because like in all great literature there is something to learn here, this book is a wonderful study of the relation between the individual and the authority of governance-we all have relations to authority in our lives</span>
  <span id="freeText17645361032852961063" style="display:none">The reason to read this book is pretty clear, first its beautiful, its a slow meditative beautiful story of a fictional past which for me is also a world far away, each page is slow and the details are full of color and light, sighing and the distant sounds of brooks<br />but secondly because like in all great literature there is something to learn here, this book is a wonderful study of the relation between the individual and the authority of governance-we all have relations to authority in our lives, they might not be as clear as the relations in this book, but it is a great meditation on this relation and the value of authority.<br />The book also for one final note has a very personal touch, the story is like most stories somewhat based on real life, and that adds to its power<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17645361032852961063" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Marina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6633293-marina"><img alt="Marina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542885639p2/6633293.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marina" href="/user/show/6633293-marina">Marina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1580078428" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16300960311230294106">What a piece of art this book is. I could feel the main characters feelings, his anxiety, worries, fright, happiness, love for his friend, doubts. Although I haven't been in similar situations as he has, it was easy to follow his stream of thoughts. <br/>The language is unique: simple, in some parts lyrical, with some turkish-originated words.<br/>One technical part I really loved was the tempo of the text: when everything was calm, the sentences were longer and you could sense peace from memories, nature</span>
  <span id="freeText16300960311230294106" style="display:none">What a piece of art this book is. I could feel the main characters feelings, his anxiety, worries, fright, happiness, love for his friend, doubts. Although I haven't been in similar situations as he has, it was easy to follow his stream of thoughts. <br />The language is unique: simple, in some parts lyrical, with some turkish-originated words.<br />One technical part I really loved was the tempo of the text: when everything was calm, the sentences were longer and you could sense peace from memories, nature in the narrative part. But, when the main charakter was in action, you could feel his stress and excitement from the short sentences written as he was short of breath. Great peace of our culture!</span>

  <span id="freeText3320018957779158" style="display:none">It wasn't easy to grade this book grade 4.<br />I did it mainly because it is book written from the first person where main protagonists thoughts are a primary source of storytelling and I felt it was kinda too long. <br />I do like builts up toward story but I was kinda overwhelmed here, don't know maybe I am not used to this kind of storytelling.<br />But this book is timeless, you can find topics covered here thought the whole history and no doubt they will be found into the future. <br />I feel kinda bad giving this book 4, but somehow it wouldn't be right, I didn't enjoy it enough for 5.<br />Nevertheless amazing classic and very good book.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('261jWKV6oM7oOIbrCriWob6L8hz4ExCvgbIy4FqM2j7hiwqKHiRppk8TMor9TZx+aNQV1DaensjazHRRl88opA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('EtDAuGoXfoCTMWXK5Bp1i2K/2eeTjyk1PNjBHRi6knQo9qlq0Um36DQa0asT739UtOA+L10Cp1Jnpoes1flg7g==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_11553583'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11553583-prokleta-avlija?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Prokleta avlija<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2147.Ivo_Andri_?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Ivo Andrić<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.30 avg rating &mdash; 7,169 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1954\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer16792483357498787772\">Prokleta avlija/The Damned Yard (Description from Ivo Andrić Foundation website)\n\nThe novel is written in 1954. Ćamil, a wealthy young man of Smyrna living in the last years of the Ottoman Empire, is fascinated by the story of Džem, ill-fated brother<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16792483357498787772\" style=\"display:none\">Prokleta avlija/The Damned Yard (Description from Ivo Andrić Foundation website)\n\nThe novel is written in 1954. Ćamil, a wealthy young man of Smyrna living in the last years of the Ottoman Empire, is fascinated by the story of Džem, ill-fated brother of the Sultan Bajazet, who ruled Turkey in the fifteenth century. Ćamil, in his isolation, comes to believe that he is Džem, and that he shares his evil destiny: he is born to be a victim of the State. Because of his stories about Džem’s ambitions to overthrow his brother, Ćamil is arrested under suspicion of plotting against the Sultan. He is taken to a prison in Istanbul, where he tells his story, to Petar, a monk.\n\nOut of these exotic materials, Andrić has constructed a book of great clarity, brevity and interest. No doubt it will be read by some as a political parable about the tyranny of the State, but also as a quite simply story about ill-fortune and human misunderstanding, fear and ignorance. Džem and Ćamil are doomed – and the certainty of their persecution is sometimes relieved, sometimes intensified by the stupidity and fright of the people who cross their ill-starred lives.\n\nConstruction takes up most of the book’s space: the central story of Džem as related by Ćamil lasts only a chapter or two. For the rest of the time the reader strips layer off layer, as one narrator passes him on the next. There is an interesting passage that helps to explain this method, at the moment when Ćamil starts narrating Džem’s story in the first person. “I” is a word, we are told, which fixes the position of the speaker in such a way that the exercise of will is no longer possible, and the speaker strength is exceeded – strength, presumably, to break out of the identification that all his past actions and thoughts force upon him when he uses the word. “I” is both a confession and an imprisonment. The fact that the novel passes the reader on from one narrator to the next rather suggests that the author is taking constant evasive action, lest he betray himself or his reader into the kind of “personal confession” which seals the fate of Ćamil. What exactly this game of form flirting with meaning signifies, must be left to the individual reader.\n\nThe movement is centripetal, towards Džem’s story, and then disperses. Details within the story are made to mimic this form. Thus when Peter receives the message telling him of his impending release:\n\n“Two younger prisoners...were chasing around using him as the centerpoint of ever narrowing circles. Annoyed, he tried to break away from these exuberant youths when one of them brushed against him and he felt a folded scrap of paper thrust into his hand. The youths continued their chase but now in widening circles...”\n\nThe reader is led on just such a chase in the course of the novel. The effect of this is to make the plot seem more like a poetic image than an ordinary plot: capable, therefore, of as many meanings as are the images of an allusive poem. Yet the language is simple and direct, not at all “poetic”. The characters are remarkable alive, even in conversation. Karađoz, the governor of the goal, is a spidery authoritarian, who loves to torment the charges he loves. The prisoners “complained about the way one complains about one’s life and curses one’s destiny...it would have been hard for them to imagine life without him”.\n\n“The Devil’s Yard” is justified, as all symbolic and figurative novels must be, by the extent to which it touches the emotions. It is extremely moving. Fear, horror, despair, amusement at times – all these indicate that the threat of the meaning has been recognized.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16792483357498787772\" href=\"#\" Učitelj je jako maltretirao i tukao djecu tako da su djeca počela bježati od škole. Vođa dječaka je bio Jovanče a osim njega tu su bili Lazar Mačak, Stric, Đoko Potrk, Nikolca prikolca i djevojčica Lunja. \n\nU Prokinom gaju oni su napravili jedan logor pod nazivom Tepsija. Tu su bježali iz škole i družili se po čitav dan. Jednog dana Lazar Mačak i Jovanče su otkrili prolaz u jednu podzemnu špilju. Kada se učitelj paprika požalio Knezu da djeca ne dolaze u školu, zajedno sa poljarem Lijanom i roditeljima organizirali su potjeru i pronašli su skrovište djece. \nZahvaljujući što su sreli Nikoletinu Bursaća oslobađaju djecu i odluče da odu u vareš da pronađu učiteljicu Lanu. Uskoro počinje rat. Dječaci koji su se do jučer igrali postaju aktivni učesnici i svjedoci tog rata. Oni pomažu partizanima i otkrivaju im tajnu pećinu. Oni učestvuju u obrani svog sela zajedno sa borcima i postaju partizanski kuriri. Na kraju odlaze u rat s nadom da će se ponovo vratiti u Prokinom gaju i u svoj logor Tepsiju.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14717599311311603581\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/205563.Me_a_Selimovi_"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1245971103p3/205563.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/205563.Me_a_Selimovi_">Meša Selimović
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
693
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11819294802902943181">Mehmed "Meša" Selimović was a Yugoslav and a Bosnian writer from Bosnia and Herzegovina and one of the greatest Bosnian writers of the 20th century. His most famous works deal with Bosnia and Herzegovina and the culture of the Bosniak inhabitants of the Ottoman province of Bosnia.</span>

</div>
